data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high-rate%20performance">high-rate performance</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the continuously growing energy consumption, the development of novel large-scale energy storage equipment with superior energy and power density to meet ever-increasing social demand has become urgent and imperative. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Hybrid ion capacitors (HICs), a kind of newly developed energy storage device, constructed with a bulk intercalation ion storage anode (high-energy battery electrode) and anion adsorption cathode (high-power capacitor electrode), have attracted extensive interest in recent years. Ascribed to the hybrid energy storage mechanism, HICs generally exhibit a larger power density compared with the traditional lithium-ion battery and sodium-ion battery and higher energy density than electrochemical capacitors. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 4 to 8">(4−8)</a> When using Li<sup>+</sup> or Na<sup>+</sup> and corresponding anion such as ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> as charge carries, the equipment is called lithium-ion capacitors (LICs) or sodium-ion capacitors (SICs), respectively. Specifically, owing to the significantly lower cost of sodium resources compared to lithium, SICs have gradually gained popularity among researchers. More interestingly, contrary to general views, the solvated sodium ion exhibits a smaller size than solvated lithium ions; consequently, it exhibits higher ionic conductivity and lower viscosity, which make the sodium-ion storage devices have potential to achieve faster kinetics characteristics than lithium-ion storage devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 9 to 12">(9−12)</a> As suggested by recent reports, some sodium-ion storage anode materials exhibit capacitor-like energy storage features with total sloping charging–discharging curves because of the easier ion diffusion process in their electrolyte solution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 to 16">(13−16)</a> While, this makes SIC more appealing than LIC for high-performance energy storage in the future, the practical utilization of SICs is still hindered by several challenges, such as insufficient energy capability and poor cycling stability. Fundamentally, the major bottleneck in the development of SICs is the lack of sufficient insertion-type sodium-ion storage anode materials to meet the ultrafast kinetics of the adsorption cathode energy-storage process during the charge–discharge process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref17" aria-label="reference 12 and 17">(12,17)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Considering the low Na<sup>+</sup> storage potential, superior chemical stability, natural abundance, and environmental friendliness, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, which features a zigzag layer of titanium oxygen octahedrons and is linked by edges, is always considered to be promising anode material for SIC among all kinds of sodium storage anode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 18 to 21">(18−21)</a> However, in order to satisfy the principle of lowest energy, the sodium ions preferably migrate along these zigzag layers, rather than travelling through these layers, which greatly limits Na<sup>+</sup> insertion–extraction in the conventional Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Additionally, ascribed to the irreversible mechanical strain upon Na<sup>+</sup> uptake into the lattice, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> exhibits undesirable cycling stability for continuous Na<sup>+</sup> storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 23 to 25">(23−25)</a> Consequently, sluggish kinetics characteristics and inferior cycling stability storage limit the electrochemical performance of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> as a Na<sup>+</sup> storage anode material. In recent years, engineering unique Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanostructures to shorten the length of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths and then improve Na<sup>+</sup> insertion–extraction ability of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> has attracted extensive attention. For instance, Bao et al. reported sodium titanate nanoribbons which exhibit superior kinetics because of the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion–extraction ability, benefiting from their unique structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Furthermore, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanotube arrays reported by the Li group display superior cycling stability for Na<sup>+</sup> storage ascribed to the surface modification and unique three-dimensional (3D) array structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> However, currently, there is still limited reports about the rational and controllable design of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanostructures for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, which limits its rate performance, cycling stability, and further applications.</div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, the fact that the sodium ions can migrate much easily along the (001) facet of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> layers compared to other facets has been demonstrated <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Thus, by shortening the length or width of two-dimensional (2D) Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> layers into a one-dimensional (1D) layered structure with the dominated 001 facet to reduce the diffusion distance is desirable for fast sodium-ion insertion–extraction. Meanwhile, designing hollow nanostructured anode materials for improved sodium-ion storage capability has also attracted extraordinary attention because the hollow nanostructure can effort a high accessible specific surface area for the electrolyte and abundant reaction active sites <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 7 and 27 to 29">(7,27−29)</a> Therefore, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> with hollow architecture assembled from (001) facet-dominated narrow ultrathin layers is expected to exhibit superior rate performance for sodium-ion storage. Although using hollow Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> as sodium-ion storage anode materials presents numerous advantages, and the rigid and brittle porous shell and huge cavity originated from the hollow architecture lead to poor structural stability (easily cracked during the drastic reaction process), which results in relatively unsatisfactory cycling stability for sustaining Na<sup>+</sup> storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> Besides engineering hollow nanostructures, hierarchical 3D structures have also been widely employed in sodium-ion storage anode materials because of their superior structure stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 30, 32 and 33">(30,32,33)</a> Consequently, transforming hollow Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> into a hierarchical 3D hollow structure may be an effective strategy to enhance the hollow architecture stability and then achieve superior cycling stability. Unfortunately, controllable preparation of hierarchical 3D hollow Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> architectures remains challenging and has not yet been achieved.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, based on the above consideration, hierarchical 3D interconnected urchin-like hollow Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> chains (Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH) was synthesized using a facile and simplified template-assisting method. Furthermore, through a variety of controlled experiments, we explored how to effectively engineer the direction of crystal growth and string the hierarchical spheres together. As expected, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH can deliver an excellent reversible capacity of 96.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> during the ultrafast charge–discharge process of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which is much more superior to the control specimens in this work and other reported results about Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> in the recent literature, suggesting that hollow architecture with the dominated (001) facet exhibit huge superiority for fast Na<sup>+</sup> storage. Meanwhile, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcHU also displayed excellent cycling stability with 15% capacity loss at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles, suggesting that the assembly of the urchin-like hollow structure into the interconnected crossed “urchin” chains can release the strain and prevent the structure from collapsing during the drastic electrochemical reaction process. Finally, by coupling the Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH anode and a commercial active carbon (CAC) cathode, a SIC was successfully fabricated and exhibited a broad voltage window (1–4 V) and high energy storage densities of 134.3 and 38.2 W h kg<sup>–1</sup> at power densities of 125 and 2500 W kg<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The SIC device also presented high capacity retention of 75% at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 charge–discharge cycles. The outstanding electrochemical performance of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH and the exploration of its growth mechanism presented in this work offer a promising basis for designing and future research on energy storage materials.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Synthesis</h3><div id="sec2_1_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.1.1.  Synthesis of SiO<sub>2</sub></h4><div class="NLM_p last">SiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres were prepared by the typical Stöber method. Typically, 9 mL of ammonia aqueous solution (28 wt %) was added to 41 mL of an ethanol/water mixture (ethanol/water ratio = 1.5:1) with stirring for 30 min. Then, a mixture that consists of 4.5 mL of tetraethyl orthosilicate (TEOS) and 45.5 mL of absolute ethanol was quickly added into the above solution. Finally, the mixture was vigorously stirred at room temperature for 5 h to obtain SiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres.</div></div><div id="sec2_1_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.1.2.  Synthesis of Ti(OH)<sub><i>x</i></sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub></h4><div class="NLM_p last">Typically, 1 g of SiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres was ultrasonically dispersed in a mixed solution (81 mL of ethanol, 4.2 mL of ammonia, 1.6 mL of water, and 27 mL of acetonitrile) at 4 °C. Then, a mixed solution containing 10 mL of acetonitrile, 35 mL of ethanol, and 1 mL of titanium tetraisopropanolate (TTIP) was dropwise added into the above SiO<sub>2</sub> colloidal solution at 4 °C under continuous stirring. Subsequently, the obtained mixture was stirred at 4 °C for 12 h. Finally, the Ti(OH)<sub><i>x</i></sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub> was collected by centrifugation, washed with ethanol three times, and dried at 60 °C for 12 h.</div></div><div id="sec2_1_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.1.3.  Synthesis of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH</h4><div class="NLM_p last">As-prepared white powder Ti(OH)<sub><i>x</i></sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub> (50 mg) was ultrasonically dispersed in a mixed solution containing 0.5 mL of 30% H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> aqueous solution and 30 mL of 1 M NaOH aqueous solution at room temperature. After that, the mixture was transferred into a 50 mL Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave and kept at 160 °C for 12 h. Finally, the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH was collected by centrifugation, washed with deionized water three times, and dried at 60 °C for 12 h.</div></div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.2.  Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, JSM-7800F) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL2100) were used to observe the morphology of the as-prepared sample. The crystal structure information of the as-prepared specimens was collected by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Shimadzu-7000) with Cu Kα radiation. The textural property was investigated using an Autosorb-1 (Quantachrome Instruments). The specific surface area was calculated by the multiple point Brunauer–Emmett–Teller model.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div id="sec2_3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">2.3.1.  Fabrication of Half Cells</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrodes were prepared by a slurry coating procedure. Typically, the electrodes consisted of 70 wt % active material, 20 wt % acetylene black, and 10 wt % polyvinylidene fluoride. The abovementioned powder was mixed by using <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone, and after that, abovementioned slurry was uniformly coated on a clean and polished copper foil. After drying, the working electrode was employed to a package button battery in the argon-filled glovebox. The glass fiber was used as the separator. The mixture of 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate and diethyl carbonate in a 50:50 volume ratio with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate electrolyte was used as the electrolyte. The sodium metal was used as the counter electrode. The button battery were galvanostatically cycled in a voltage window of 0.01–2.5 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) for the anode and 2.0–4.2 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) for the cathode on the LAND CT2001 tester. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were recorded on the CHI760e electrochemical workstation (Shanghai Chenhua). The specific capacity was calculated based on the mass of active materials and mass loading is around 1 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The specific capacity (mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) is calculated by <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">eq S1</a>.</div></div><div id="sec2_3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">2.3.2.  Fabrication of SIC</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The CAC electrode (HF-Kejing Co, Ltd) and the Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH was used as the cathode active materials and anode active materials, respectively. The glass fiber was used as the separator. The electrolyte in the SIC is the same with that in half cells. The current density was based on the total mass of both the electrode active materials, and the average loading of the anodes was around 1 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Before assembling SIC, the Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH anode was pretested three cycles at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> in a half cell. The specific capacity (F g<sup>–1</sup>), energy density (W h kg<sup>–1</sup>), and power density (W kg<sup>–1</sup>) of SIC are calculated by <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">eqs S2, S4, and S5</a>.</div></div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.1.  Composition and Structure Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">The assembly and synthesis process for simplified template-derived Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH is schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a and the experimental details are also presented in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. First, smooth and uniform SiO<sub>2</sub> spheres with about 400 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a>) were successfully prepared following a modified Stöber method. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Subsequently, amorphous Ti(OH)<sub><i>x</i></sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) was uniformly coated on the surface of SiO<sub>2</sub> with an average thickness of about 9 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3</a>). Finally, the hierarchical 3D interconnected urchin-like hollow Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH) were directly produced by H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>-assisted simultaneous alkalization of Ti(OH)<sub><i>x</i></sub> and SiO<sub>2</sub> in a one-step hydrothermal reaction system. In the proposed simplified hard-template strategy, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> is skillfully grown with the SiO<sub>2</sub> template being removed at the same time, which eliminates unnecessary and manipulative steps and also allows the possibility to obtain materials with unique morphology.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/medium/am9b14560_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration of the route for synthesis of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH. (b–d) FESEM images of the SiO<sub>2</sub> template, Ti(OH)<sub><i>x</i></sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH, respectively. (e–g) TEM images of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH with different magnification.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b14560&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The microstructure and morphology of as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH was further characterized by FESEM and TEM. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4a</a>, after the hydrothermal reaction, Ti(OH)<sub><i>x</i></sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub> was successfully transformed into urchin-like Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> spheres. More interestingly, these uniform spheres are connected and form well-defined interconnected chain structures. Then, the detailed morphology and internal information was further observed in TEM images. As shown by the low-magnification TEM images of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4b</a>, the interconnected chain structure was further confirmed, and the internal hollow structure of the “urchin” is clearly visible. It is pertinent to mention that the hollow core of these “urchins” has a smaller size (about 300 nm) than the original SiO<sub>2</sub> template (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a>), which may be derived from the unique experimental method that combines the growth of the Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> crystal with the removal of the hard template into one step, leading to the free growth of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images, displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f,g, further demonstrate that the “urchins” consist of arrays of highly uniform, slender, and vertical 1D nanoneedles with a thickness of less than 10 nm. Meanwhile, a lattice spacing of 0.78 nm [(001) facet] is observed on the nanoneedles (the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f).</div><div class="NLM_p">It was confusing to find that when being without H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, the Ti(OH)<sub><i>x</i></sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub>-derived Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> exhibits dispersed hollow sphere structures that consisted of 2D nanosheets (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>; denoted as Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH), which is a great difference compared to the Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH. In order to fully understand the forming mechanism of the unique structure of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH, a variety of control experiments were designed and carried out to investigate the influence of vital parameters (TTIP, TEOS, and H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) on the microstructure of the corresponding products (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). Based on <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S6–S8</a> and corresponding discussion in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, a reasonable mechanism for the formation of the unique structure of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH under hydrothermal conditions is proposed and schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. First, H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> can promote the Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> crystal growth toward 1D direction and then assembled into an urchin-like nanosphere. Second, these urchin-like nanospheres can connect and form a 3D interconnected chain structure when sodium silicate, originating from SiO<sub>2</sub> or TEOS, is presented in the above system.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/medium/am9b14560_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a,b) Probable role of H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and sodium silicate during the growth process of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> under the hydrothermal condition by using TTIP as the precursor.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b14560&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to better understand the effect of H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> on the crystal growth of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, the crystal structure of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH was characterized by using an X-ray diffractometer (XRD). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, all the diffraction peaks of the resulting products are well indexed into the layered Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (PDF#31-1329) with clearly visible (001), (011), (300), and (020) sharp peaks. However, it should be pointed out that Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH exhibits weaker diffraction peaks, especially for the layered diffraction peak at about 10°, compared to Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH. Meanwhile, from <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, when using TTIP as the precursor, the resulting products also exhibit the same phenomenon that the H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>-assisted prepared Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> exhibit stronger diffraction peaks compared to the other. This further demonstrates that H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> can manipulate the crystal growth of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, especially for the layered (001) lattice plane, and then obtained superior crystallinity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/medium/am9b14560_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD pattern of the obtained Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH. (b) Crystal structure of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b14560&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The hollow structure properties of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH was also revealed by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH exhibits an obvious and big H3-type hysteresis loop, indicating the typical mesoporous characteristic and existence of the internal cave, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref37" aria-label="reference 12 and 37">(12,37)</a> which is consistent with the hollow structure of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH. For comparison, the textural properties of solid urchin-like Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (prepared by using TTIP as the precursor) was also measured under the same conditions and shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>. Clearly, the solid urchin-like Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> displayed a similar adsorption–desorption isotherm to that of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH but presented a much smaller hysteresis loop. From calculation, the solid urchin-like Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> exhibits a similar specific surface area (92.015 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) with Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH (99.078 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) but possesses a smaller total pore volume (0.249 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) than Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH (0.384 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>). This result demonstrates that the hollow structure can produce large pore volumes and it is beneficial for rapid sodium-ion transmission. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">3.2.  Sodium-Ion Storage Properties in the Half Cells</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH measured by coupling with metallic sodium electrodes is exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. The CV curves at various sweep rates from 0.1 to 4 mV s<sup>–1</sup> are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, which reveal an anodic peak around 0.68 V and a cathodic peak around 0.25 V, ascribed to the sodium-ion extraction–insertion in Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. In order to evaluate the sodium-ion storage kinetics of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH, the surface capacitive contribution (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and diffusion-controlled contribution (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) at specific potential were quantified and separated from total current response by the following equation<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/medium/am9b14560_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i15"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>v</i> is the sweep rate and <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are adjustable parameters that determine the current response from the surface capacitive and diffusion contribution at a specific potential. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 38 to 41">(38−41)</a> As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b (the calculation methods and details are provided in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> and Figure S11), the proportion of surface capacitive to the total current response increases with increasing scan rate and finally reaches a high occupation proportion of 84% at 4 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, which suggests that Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH possess an approximate pseudocapacitive sodium-ion storage kinetics characteristic.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/medium/am9b14560_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) CV curves of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH at various scan rates from 0.1 to 4 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Capacitive contributions of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH to the total current response at different scan rates. (c) Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH with increasing current density from 0.05 to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate performance of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH. (e) Long-term cycling performance of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b14560&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The initial galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage window of 0.01–2.5 V are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>; it is obvious that Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH exhibits a superior reversible discharge capacity of 225 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with complete sloping curves. However, the Coulombic efficiency of 48% in the first cycle is relativity low, which may be attributed to the decomposition of the electrolyte and the formation of the passivation layer on the surface of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 and 16">(13,16)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To evaluate the rate capability of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH, the galvanostatic charge–discharge at various current densities was also examined (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c; corresponding specific capacities are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). It is apparent that the capacity for sodium-ion storage is mainly from below 1.5 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) with consistent sloping curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), which is beneficial for assembling high energy density SICs. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, with increasing current density from 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2 to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH delivered excellent reversible capacities from 205.7, 183.4, 164.3, 149.7, 135.5, 125.8 to 96.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH can still deliver a superior reversible capacity of 181.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density was abruptly changed back to 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">In a sharp contrast, the rate capabilities of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH are also presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. The dispersive 2D nanosheet-assembled hollow Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH exhibits rapid capacity decay at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> and only about 30% of the reversible capacity of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Obviously, the rapid capacity decay of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH during the initial process is resulting from the brittle and unstable hollow structure, however, it is confusing why hollow Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH exhibit much inferior rate capability than Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH. With this question, we carefully compared the XRD and HRTEM of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH with those of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH. First, we can find that the (001) facet diffraction peak of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH is much stronger than that of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a), which demonstrates that H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> can not only induce the 1D crystal-oriented growth of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> but can also induce the (001) facet preferential growth in Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Furthermore, the HRTEM image of the 1D Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanoneedles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b) reveals that the (001) facets are dominated exposed in the nanoneedles. However, for 2D nanosheets, the (011) facet is abundant and dominated in the surface of nanosheets (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). It is generally known that Na<sup>+</sup> favorably migrates along the (001) facet of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. Hence, based on the above TEM analysis, speculation about the different Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths in the 1D Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanoneedles and 2D Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanosheets is proposed and schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d,e. Obviously, ascribed to the more exposed (001) facet and shorter Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path, the slender 1D Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanoneedles exhibit faster Na<sup>+</sup> storage characteristics than 2D Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanosheets. In order to verify the above hypothesis, the sodium-ion storage kinetics of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH were evaluated by the relationship between the measured current (<i>i</i>) and sweep rate (<i>v</i>) in the following equation<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/medium/am9b14560_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i16"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where both <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable parameters, and the types of electrochemical reaction can be determined by the <i>b</i>-value. Typically, <i>b</i> = 0.5 represents a total diffusion-controlled sodium-ion storage kinetics and <i>b</i> = 1.0 symbolizes a total capacitive-controlled sodium-ion storage kinetics. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f, the <i>b</i>-value of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH for the anodic peaks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a) was quantified to be 0.912 at sweep rates from 0.1 to 4 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, indicating that the sodium-ion storage kinetics of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH can be described as a fast pseudocapacitive-controlled characteristic. Meanwhile, the <i>b</i>-value of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH for the anodic peaks from the CV curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>) was also quantified and shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>g, where the smaller <i>b</i>-value (0.676) indicates the more sluggish sodium-ion storage kinetics than Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH. In summary, it has been demonstrated that the 1D layered Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanoneedles with an exposed (001) facet can better facilitate the diffusion of sodium ions than 2D Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanosheets. Furthermore, compared with Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH, the interconnected solid urchin-like Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> exhibits inferior rate capability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>), which demonstrated that the hollow structure can also improve the rate capability for sodium-ion storage in Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/medium/am9b14560_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Crystal structure of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. (b) HRTEM image of the 1D Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanoneedle. (c) HRTEM image of the 2D Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> nanosheet. (d,e) Polyhedral representation of 1D and 2D layered Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> sodium-ion insertion–extraction schematic. (f,g) Peak current of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH plotted against the sweep rate, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b14560&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e, the long-term cycle ability of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH is demonstrated for 2000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In more detail, the specific capacity of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH displays a slow decrease from 101.8 to 85 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with an average capacity loss of 0.0075% per cycle. In contrast, the cycling performance of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH was also examined for 1000 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> and presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>. It is apparent that the cycling performance of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH was worse than Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH, which supports the idea that the interconnected chain structure can provide better cycling performance because of its superior structural stability. In order to verify the above standpoint, the morphology of the Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH electrodes after 1000 cycles was characterized and is illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>. Clearly, the interconnected chain structure of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH was maintained even after a long cycle test. However, the hollow structure of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH was completely destroyed and no hollow sphere can be observed. Consequently, it is obvious that the 3D interconnected chain structure allows a better release of strain and prevents the structure from collapsing, thus achieving superior cycling performance.</div><div class="NLM_p last">To better value the rate capabilities and cycling performance for sodium-ion storage of as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH, reported data about Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> for sodium-ion storage in the recent literature were listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. This comparison undoubtedly indicates that the as-synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH by the unique structural design in this work exhibits comparative and even more superior rate capability and cycling performance for sodium-ion storage than other reported studies about Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i17" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i17">3.3.  Electrochemical Performance of SICs</h3><div class="NLM_p">By employing the as-synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH as an insertion-type anode and CAC as an adsorption-type cathode (the pore structure and morphology are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>, and electrochemical performances in the half-battery are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>) with a mass ratio from 1:1 to 1:2.5, the SICs were assembled, and its energy storage performance was measured in the potential window of 1–4.0 V. From the Ragone plot of different mass ratios between the anode and cathode in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/suppl_file/am9b14560_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>, the SIC with a mass ratio of 1:1.5 displayed the highest energy-power density. Thus, it was employed as the optimum ratio to assemble SIC, and its detailed electrochemical performance is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>. The CV curves of the as-assembled SIC device at a scan rate between 1 and 6 mV s<sup>–1</sup> are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a, and its near-rectangular shape demonstrates a typical capacitive-like energy storage behavior. Meanwhile, typical linear capacitive-like charge–discharge profiles at different current densities from 0.05 to 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> were also obtained and exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b. The energy and power densities of an as-assembled SIC device can be calculated based on the active materials in both the electrodes from the charge–discharge profiles curves. The as-fabricated SIC device displayed a maximum energy density of 134.3 W h kg<sup>–1</sup> at a power density of 125 W kg<sup>–1</sup>, and even at a high-power output of 5000 W kg<sup>–1</sup>, it still delivered an energy density of 20 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, the long-term cycle stability of the as-assembled SIC device was evaluated <i>t</i> 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> in the potential window of 1–4 V. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c, about 75% of the initial energy density can be retained after 2000 cycles. Finally, the Ragone plots (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e) reveal that the as-assembled SIC can deliver superior energy/power density compared with other several representative SIC devices reported in recent literature studies, such as Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>@C@RGO, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> SCN-A//SCN-A, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//CDC, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//GNS, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> TiO<sub>2</sub>Mic-RGO//AC, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>//AC, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-CNT/AC. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/medium/am9b14560_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) CV curves with increasing sweep rate from 1 to 6 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and (b) galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of as-assembled SIC with a mass ratio of 1:1.5. (c) Long-term cycle performance of the as-assembled SIC at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Structure schematic of the as-assembled SIC device. (e) Ragone plot of the as-assembled SIC compared with other SIC systems in the recent literature.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b14560/asset/images/large/am9b14560_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b14560&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i18">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the unique hierarchical 3D interconnected urchin-like hollow Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> chains were fabricated by a simplified template-assisting method. Remarkably, how to controllably engineer the crystal-oriented growth and unique morphology have been explored and a reasonable forming mechanism was proposed. The as-synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH exhibited an excellent rate capability of 96.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which is attributed to the urchin-like hollow structure and dominated (001) facet. Meanwhile, by assembling these dispersed urchin-like hollow Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> spheres into a 3D interconnected chain structure, the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH can display superior stability for sustaining sodium-ion storage. As expected, by using the excellent electrochemical performance of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH as an anode and CAC as a cathode, the as-prepared SIC exhibited an exceptional energy storage performance. Thus, the unique structural design for Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and its formation mechanism exploration in this study certainly pave a way for the application of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and other layered materials in the field of energy storage. Moreover, we are firmly convinced that better electrochemical performance of as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> will be achieved if the electronic conductivity can be further improved.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i19">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b14560" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b14560</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">TEM images of the as-prepared SiO<sub>2</sub> template; XRD pattern of Ti(OH)<sub>x</sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub>; TEM images of Ti(OH)<sub>x</sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub> with different magnification; FESEM and TEM images of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcUH; FESEM and TEM images of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-SH; FESEM image of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-S, (b) Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-U, and (c) Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>-IcU;                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanomaterials for energy conversion and storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Qifeng; Uchaker, Evan; Candelaria, Stephanie L.; Cao, Guozhong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Nanostructured materials are advantageous in offering huge surface to vol. ratios, favorable transport properties, altered phys. properties, and confinement effects resulting from the nanoscale dimensions, and have been extensively studied for energy-related applications such as solar cells, catalysts, thermoelecs., lithium ion batteries, supercapacitors, and hydrogen storage systems.  This review focuses on a few select aspects regarding these topics, demonstrating that nanostructured materials benefit these applications by (1) providing a large surface area to boost the electrochem. reaction or mol. adsorption occurring at the solid-liq. or solid-gas interface, (2) generating optical effects to improve optical absorption in solar cells, and (3) giving rise to high crystallinity and/or porous structure to facilitate the electron or ion transport and electrolyte diffusion, so as to ensure the electrochem. process occurs with high efficiency.  It is emphasized that, to further enhance the capability of nanostructured materials for energy conversion and storage, new mechanisms and structures are anticipated.  In addn. to highlighting the obvious advantages of nanostructured materials, the limitations and challenges of nanostructured materials while being used for solar cells, lithium ion batteries, supercapacitors, and hydrogen storage systems have also been addressed in this review.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1149/1.1383553"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amatucci, G.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A nonaq. asym. electrochem. cell technol. is presented where the pos. electrode stores charge through a reversible non-faradaic or pseudo-capacitive reaction of anions on the surface of an activated carbon pos. electrode.  The neg. electrode is a cryst. intercalation compd. which supports the fast reversible intercalation of lithium ions; the nonaq electrolyte is LiPF6.  Using a pos. electrode material of activated carbon and newly developed neg. electrode material of nanostructured Li4Ti5O12 we obtain a cell which exhibits a sloping voltage profile from 3 to 1.5 V, 90% capacity utilization at 10C charge/discharge rates, and 10-15% capacity loss after 5000 cycles. Oxidn. during the anodic scan decreased significantly after surface passivation at high voltage and elevated temps.  We also introduce the asym. hybrid technol. in a bonded flat plate plastic cell configuration where packaged energy densities were calcd. to be in excess of 20 Wh/kg.  In addn., a practical method for three-electrode anal. of Li cells by use of a Ag quasi-ref. electrode wire is discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c4ee02986k"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Karpuzov, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kohandehghan, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mitlin, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Peanut Shell Hybrid Sodium Ion Capacitor with Extreme Energy–Power Rivals Lithium Ion Capacitors</span>. <i>Energy Environ.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Peanut shell hybrid sodium ion capacitor with extreme energy-power rivals lithium ion capacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Jia; Wang, Huanlei; Li, Zhi; Cui, Kai; Karpuzov, Dimitre; Tan, Xuehai; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">This is the first report of a hybrid sodium ion capacitor (NIC) with the active materials in both the anode and the cathode being derived entirely from a single precursor: peanut shells, which are a green and highly economical waste globally generated at over 6 million tons per yr.  The electrodes push the envelope of performance, delivering among the most promising sodiation capacity-rate capability-cycling retention combinations reported in the literature for each materials class.  Hence the resultant NIC also offers a state-of-the-art cyclically stable combination of energy and power, not only in respect to previously but also as compared to Li ion capacitors (LICs).  The ion adsorption cathode based on Peanut Shell Nanosheet Carbon (PSNC) displays a hierarchically porous architecture, a sheet-like morphol. down to 15 nm in thickness, a surface area on par with graphene materials (up to 2396 m2 g-1) and high levels of oxygen doping (up to 13.51 wt%).  Scanned from 1.5-4.2 V vs.  Na/Na+ PSNC delivers a specific capacity of 161 mA h g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 and 73 mA h g-1 at 25.6 A g-1.  A low surface area Peanut Shell Ordered Carbon (PSOC) is employed as an ion intercalation anode.  PSOC delivers a total capacity of 315 mA h g-1 with a flat plateau of 181 mA h g-1 occurring below 0.1 V (tested at 0.1 A g-1), and is stable at 10 000 cycles (tested at 3.2 A g-1).  The assembled NIC operates within a wide temp. range (0-65 °C), yielding at room temp. (by active mass) 201, 76 and 50 W h kg-1 at 285, 8500 and 16 500 W kg-1, resp.  At 1.5-3.5 V, the hybrid device achieved 72% capacity retention after 10 000 cycles tested at 6.4 A g-1, and 88% after 100 000 cycles at 51.2 A g-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1038/nmat5029"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jeżowski, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Crosnier, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deunf, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Poizot, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Béguin, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Brousse, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Safe and Recyclable Lithium-Ion Capacitors using Sacrificial Organic Lithium Salt</span>. <i>Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion capacitors (LICs) shrewdly combine a lithium-ion battery neg. electrode capable of reversibly intercalating lithium cations, namely graphite, together with an elec. double-layer pos. electrode, namely activated carbon.  However, the beauty of this concept is marred by the lack of a lithium-cation source in the device, thus requiring a specific preliminary charging step.  The strategies devised thus far in an attempt to rectify this issue all present drawbacks.  Our research uncovers a unique approach based on the use of a lithiated org. material, namely 3,4-dihydroxybenzonitrile dilithium salt.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hybrid ion capacitors, esp. sodium ion capacitors (SICs), have recently attracted enormous attention due to their combined merits of high energy d. from the battery-type anode and high power d. from the capacitor-type cathode.  However, achieving high-performance SICs to overcome the sluggish kinetic energy storage feature and inferior cycling stability of the battery-type anode remains a challenge.  In this work, N-doped porous carbon embedded with ultrasmall titanium oxynitride nanoparticles (TiOxNy/C) was developed from metal-org. frameworks (MOFs).  As the SIC anode, the as-designed TiOxNy/C exhibited a high reversible capacity (275 mA h g-1 at 50 mA g-1), ultrahigh rate capability, and superior cycling performance, which is attributed to the effective synergy between the ultrasmall TiOxNy nanoparticles and N-doped porous carbon.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Nanoscience and nanotechnol. can provide tremendous benefits to electrochem. energy storage devices, such as batteries and supercapacitors, by combining new nanoscale properties to realize enhanced energy and power capabilities.  A no. of published reports on hybrid systems are systematically reviewed in this perspective.  Several potential strategies to enhance the energy d. above that of generation-I elec. double layer capacitors (EDLC: activated carbon/activated carbon) are discussed and some fundamental issues and future directions are identified.  We suggest a new hybrid supercapacitor system that is able to meet the energy and power demands for a variety of applications, ranging from microelectronic devices to elec. vehicles, which presents itself as a breakthrough improvement. The proposed NHC can pave the way toward generation-II supercapacitor systems by taking advantage of a novel, high quality, high efficiency and inexpensive nanomaterial prepn. procedure.  With such a breakthrough in nanofabrication-nanohybridization technol., the NHC, which utilizes an ultrafast nano-cryst. Li4Ti5O12, is considered to be an alternative for conventional generation-I EDLCs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemrev.8b00116"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Paek, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mitlin, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review of Hybrid Ion Capacitors: From Aqueous to Lithium to Sodium</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Review of Hybrid Ion Capacitors: From Aqueous to Lithium to Sodium</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Jia; Hu, Wenbin; Paek, Eunsu; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">118</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6457-6498</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHREAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2665</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this crit. Review we focus on the evolution of the hybrid ion capacitor (HIC) from its early embodiments to its modern form, focusing on the key outstanding scientific and technol. questions that necessitate further in-depth study.  It may be argued that HICs began as aq. systems, based on a Faradaic oxide pos. electrode (e.g., Co3O4, RuOx) and an activated carbon ion-adsorption neg. electrode.  In these early embodiments HICs were meant to compete directly with elec. double layer capacitors (EDLCs), rather than with the much higher energy secondary batteries.  The HIC design then evolved to be based on a wide voltage (∼4.2 V) carbonate-based battery electrolyte, using an insertion titanium oxide compd. anode (Li4Ti5O12, LixTi5O12) vs. a Li ion adsorption porous carbon cathode.  The modern Na and Li architectures contain a diverse range of nanostructured materials in both electrodes, including TiO2, Li7Ti5O12, Li4Ti5O12, Na6LiTi5O12, Na2Ti3O7, graphene, hard carbon, soft carbon, graphite, carbon nanosheets, pseudocapacitor T-Nb2O5, V2O5, MXene, conversion compds. MoS2, VN, MnO, and Fe2O3/Fe3O4, cathodes based on Na3V2(PO4)3, NaTi2(PO4)3, sodium super ionic conductor (NASICON), etc.  The Ragone chart characteristics of HIC devices critically depend on their anode-cathode architectures.  Combining electrodes with the flattest capacity vs. voltage characteristics, and the largest total voltage window, yields superior energy.  Unfortunately "flat voltage" materials undergo significant vol. expansion/contraction during cycling and are frequently lifetime limited.  Overall more research on HIC cathodes is needed; apart from high surface area carbon, very few pos. electrodes demonstrate the necessary 10 000 or 100 000 plus cycle life.  It remains to be detd. whether its lithium ion capacitors (LICs) or sodium ion capacitors (NICs) are superior in terms of energy-power and cyclability.  We discuss unresolved issues, including poorly understood fast-charge storage mechanisms, prelithiation and presodiation, solid electrolyte interface (SEI) formation, and high-rate metal plating.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201702093"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chao, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, H.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Pseudocapacitive behaviours of Na2Ti3O7@CNT coaxial nanocables for high-performance sodium-ion capacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dong, Shengyang; Shen, Laifa; Li, Hongsen; Nie, Ping; Zhu, Yaoyao; Sheng, Qi; Zhang, Xiaogang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hybrid sodium-ion capacitors (NICs) have tremendous potential in large-scale energy storage applications due to their low cost, long lifetime and high power.  However, it remains a great challenge to find a desirable anode material with fast kinetics and superior cycle life.  Here an applicable strategy to in situ grow Na2Ti3O7 on 1D CNTs as an anode material for sodium-ion capacitors is presented.  Benefiting from the unique 1D nanostructure and the presence of pseudocapacitive charge storage mechanism, the Na2Ti3O7@CNT electrode exhibits excellent electrochem. performance with high rate capability and superb cycling stability.  Moreover, a high performance hybrid NIC is also fabricated by using Na2Ti3O7@CNTs as an anode and activated carbon derived from the outer peanut shell as a cathode, which delivers high energy d. (58.5 W h kg-1), high power d. (3000 W kg-1), and long term cycle life (retaining ca. 75% of its original capacity at 0.4 A g-1 after 4000 cycles).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2017.03.036"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">An, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Novel Layer-by-Layer Stacked VS<sub>2</sub> Nanosheets with Intercalation Pseudocapacitance for High-Rate Sodium Ion Charge Storage</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>35</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">396</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">404</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Novel layer-by-layer stacked VS2 nanosheets with intercalation pseudocapacitance for high-rate sodium ion charge storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Ruimin; Wei, Qiulong; Sheng, Jinzhi; Shi, Changwei; An, Qinyou; Liu, Sijie; Mai, Liqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">35</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as a promising candidate for large scale energy storage systems due to their low cost and reasonable performance.  However, developing desirable anode materials with high capacity, excellent cycling stability and high rate capability remains great challenges.  Herein, novel layer-by-layer VS2 stacked nanosheets (VS2-SNSs) are synthesized using a facile one-step polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) assisted assembly method.  With a highly stable orderly stacked layer structure, the VS2 delivers excellent electrochem. performance in SIBs.  A reversible discharge capacity of 250 mA h g-1 is obtained at 0.2 A g-1.  A high specific capacity of 150 mA h g-1 even at 20 A g-1 is reached (discharged/charged in 27 s).  Furthermore, the VS2-SNSs deliver long cycling life with almost no capacity fading even after 600 cycles at 5 A g-1.  In-situ XRD and ex-situ TEM characterization reveal that VS2 undertakes a reversible intercalation reaction mechanism.  A detailed electrochem. kinetic anal. reveals that the sodium ion charge storage depends on intercalation pseudocapacitive behavior with a high capacitive contribution up to 69% for the total capacity at 1 mV s-1.  The impressive electrochem. performance reveals that the VS2 has great potential for the next generation large scale energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201602654"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thangavel, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moorthy, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na2Ti3O7 works as an effective low-voltage insertion sodium compd. because of its ability to reversibly uptake 2 Na ions per formula unit (200 mA-h/g) at an av. potential of 0.3 V.  This is the first ever reported oxide to reversibly react with sodium at such a low potential.  Several improvements to the present work are immediately apparent and range from electrode optimization to the detn. of the precise sodium insertion mechanism.  Nevertheless, we believe that the result reported in this paper provides great opportunities in the development of room-temp. high-performing sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1039/c2ta01057g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rudola, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saravanan, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mason, C. Through optimization of the electrolyte and binder, the microsized Na2Ti3O7 electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 188 mA h g-1 in 1 M NaFSI/PC electrolyte at a current rate of 0.1C in a voltage range of 0.0-3.0 V, with sodium alginate as binder.
